Mouthpiece Work / Effects of ramp height
FROM: frymorgan (Morgan)
SUBJECT: Effects of ramp height
I and I think most others have observed that a lower ramp decreases resistance and gives a somewhat brighter and maybe less centered sound.  Any idea what the reason(s) is?  I'm not crazy about Ferron's explanation, seems to be treating the wavefront as if it were perpendicular to the reed, which doesn't sound right to me.  How much is acoustic, how much is aerodynamic?  Some of it must be from the larger throat, but I don't think it can be all this.

Anybody have any thoughts on this?
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
Nederveen describes the oscillative flow entering the instrument as being, "composed of the oscillative flow Us1 through the slit and the oscillative flow due to the movement of the reed.   U = Us1 - Sr dh/dt", where the reed movement is simply subtracted from and limits the flow, depending upon it's position.  That would not support Ferron's assumption.  Remember, during a regime, the reed motion is coupled to the air column's air pressure fluctuations.  The air pressure moves the reed.  Not visa versa. Then the reed valve position limits the flow.  No regime, the reed stays shut.

That said, what happens between the initial attack and the point where a successful regime is formed, before the reed's motion becomes coupled with the air column air pressure, may be an entirely different matter.  In the end, Ferron is left to explain why Guardalla mouthpieces respond so well, when by his reasoning, they shouldn't.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
We have ramps simply because the designer desires to have a table with a certain thickness.  One of the nicest sounding/playing pieces I ever had (after doing some chamber modifications), was a metal Colletto.  The throat bore entered the chamber at a very low angle, and the table wall was very, very thin at the window, and just thick enough at the shank, to handle the stress of holding the mouthpiece on the cork.  It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist. 

As Benade said, an unimportant part of the air column doesn't exist, meaning, every mm affects the sound/response of the instrument in some way.  IMO, having space at the end of the window/in front of a high ramp, on a mouthpiece with a thick table, is a mistake, and rounding/angling the ramp, while reducing the obstacle, only enlarges the perturbation.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

Toby

 enlarges the perturbation.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in the reed channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
You are right that reed channel effects would not directly influence things at the ramp, but I have yet to see any definitive numerical work on the mpc chamber, and what I have read is that things in that area are poorly understood.. As Scavone notes, changes in that area would tend to affect tone color, by influencing the impedances of higher harmonics. Wyman talks a lot about how the ramp angle changes response, and it is not clear why that should be so if we are simply talking about perturbation theory, since in that case only the cross-sectional volume should matter, as the bend in the air column would be insignificant acoustically. I wonder if there is some sort of reflection set up at the end of the window that affects the reed behavior. 

It might be interesting to make the window very short: starting just at the break, and make the ramp extremely smooth and gradual. One could do so pretty easily using epoxy putty. If I had two identical mpcs I would give it a try, but with a single mpc, trying to gauge the before/after accurately would be difficult. 

I may try it anyway, if I ever have a break from reporting about the Japan disaster.

Toby

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
"It might be interesting to make the window very short: starting just at the break, and make the ramp extremely
 smooth and gradual. One could do so pretty easily using epoxy putty."

Been there, done that - with a removable brass, window-shaped insert.  Thus, I say what I say.  All my mouthpieces have the ramp extended to the break, resulting in a reduced window length having a reverse "U" shape, which parallels the mouthpiece tip rail.  I reported on it 2 years ago on MouthpieceWork.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

--- In MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

>

> Nederveen describes the oscillative flow entering the instrument as being, "composed of the oscillative flow Us1 through the slit and the oscillative flow due to the movement of the reed.Â Â  U = Us1 - Sr dh/dt", where the reed movement is simply subtracted from and limits the flow, depending upon it's position.Â  That would not support Ferron's assumption.Â  Remember, during a regime, the reed motion is coupled to the air column's air pressure fluctuations.Â  The air pressure moves the reed.Â  Not visa versa. Then the reed valve position limits the flow.Â  No regime, the reed stays shut.

> 

> That said, what happens between the initial attack and the point where a successful regime is formed, before the reed's motion becomes coupled with the air column air pressure, may be an entirely different matter.Â  In the end, Ferron is left to explain why Guardalla mouthpieces respond so well, when by his reasoning, they shouldn't.

>       I and I think most others have observed that a lower ramp decreases resistance and gives a somewhat brighter and maybe less centered sound.  Any idea what the reason(s) is?  I'm not crazy about Ferron's explanation, seems to be treating the wavefront as if it were perpendicular to the reed, which doesn't sound right to me.  How much is acoustic, how much is aerodynamic?  Some of it must be from the larger throat, but I don't think it can be all this.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
Since to a first approximation shape doesn't matter, I wonder what you mean by "function acoustically". We know that sharp edges cause turbulence and reflections and that those are undesirable, but beyond that...?

With shakuhachi flutes, some of the best-playing ones look like rough cobblestone roads inside.

--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:
> Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.
>

>       
>       You are right that reed channel effects would not directly influence things at the ramp, but I have yet to see any definitive numerical work on the mpc chamber, and what I have read is that things in that area are poorly understood.. As Scavone notes, changes in that area would tend to affect tone color, by influencing the impedances of higher harmonics. Wyman talks a lot about how the ramp angle changes response, and it is not clear why that should be so if we are simply talking about perturbation theory, since in that case only the cross-sectional volume should matter, as the bend in the air column would be insignificant acoustically. I wonder if there is some sort of reflection set up at the end of the window that affects the reed behavior. 
> 
> It might be interesting to make the window very short: starting just at the break, and make the ramp extremely
>  smooth and gradual. One could do so pretty easily using epoxy putty. If I had two identical mpcs I would give it a try, but with a single mpc, trying to gauge the before/after accurately would be difficult. 
> 
> I may try it anyway, if I ever have a break from reporting about the Japan disaster.
> 
> Toby

> --- On Mon, 2011/6/13, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:
>

>

>       
>       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."
> 
> Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in
>  the reed
>  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."
> 
> This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.
> 
> 
> 
> --- On Sun, 6/12/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:
> 
> From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
> Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height

> 
> 
> 
>  
> 
> 
> 
>     
>       
>       
>       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.
> 
> Toby
>

>       We have ramps simply because the designer desires to have a table with a certain thickness.  One of the nicest sounding/playing pieces I ever had (after doing some chamber modifications), was a metal Colletto.  The throat bore entered the chamber at a very low angle, and the table wall was very, very thin at the window, and just thick enough at the shank, to handle the stress of holding the mouthpiece on the cork.  It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist. 
> 
> As Benade said, an unimportant part of the air column doesn't exist, meaning, every mm affects the sound/response of the instrument in some way.  IMO, having space at the end of the window/in front of a high ramp, on a mouthpiece with a thick table, is a mistake, and rounding/angling the ramp, while reducing the
>  obstacle, only
>  enlarges the perturbation. 
>

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 

Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 

If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.

There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 

John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."
> 
> > 
> 
> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in
> 
> >  the reed
> 
> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."
> 
> > 
> 
> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
"In wind instrument bores the primary mode of wave propagation is along the central axis of the tube." - Scavone.   

Mathematical modeling of irregular instrument bores is "well approximated" by a series of cylindrical and conical sections.  This modeling redistributes the volume of perturbations equally around the central axis of the air column.  While this approximation may satisfy theoreticians as to the perceived nature of reality, you will never convince a mouthpiece maker that a drastic enlargement in the side of the mouthpiece wall, resulting in a 2+ mm high, perpendicular obstruction upon entering the throat, is as efficient as an equivalent, though evenly distributed enlargement of the mouthpiece chamber around the central axis, resulting in a slight, smooth contraction of the bore upon entering the throat.  A 2+ mm deep enlargement in the side of the mouthpiece is equivalent to a closed tone hole.  Hmmm.

"Well, just remove the wall with a file.", you say.  As Morgan noticed, this removes the resistance caused by the ramp wall, but causes the mouthpiece to become less centered.   Small wonder.  The chamber is now larger, the width/length ratio is changed, and, if the mouthpiece volume/frequency was matched optimally to that particular instrument/player before, it's acoustic efficiency is now reduced.   You now have more volume in that area than you would like.  You have to start over and redesign the mouthpiece to get the original sound you wanted, without the resistance.  In the end, you will have a mouthpiece with as little cross-sectional window volume and obstruction as possible and that original window volume, distributed equally around the central axis of the chamber bore.

In this case, I would consider the saxophone mouthpiece makers to be the experts, not the theoreticians nor the shak makers, nor those who admire them.  There's nothing like having the actual, real experience.  





--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



  


    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.



--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@yahoo.com> wrote:

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

>

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

> > >       I and I think most others have observed that a lower ramp decreases resistance and gives a somewhat brighter and maybe less centered sound.  Any idea what the reason(s) is?  I'm not crazy about Ferron's explanation, seems to be treating the wavefront as if it were perpendicular to the reed, which doesn't sound right to me.  How much is acoustic, how much is aerodynamic?  Some of it must be from the larger throat, but I don't think it can be all this.

...and, perhaps you would be kind enough to quote the context where Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence.  



--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



  


    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.



--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@yahoo.com> wrote:

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

> 

> > 

> 

> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in

> 

> >  the reed

> 

> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

> 

> > 

> 

> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
The question is, as always, just what that experience is worth. No way I am going to invite Antoine Lefebvre to this group to put up with you.

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



  


    
      
      
      "In wind instrument bores the primary mode of wave propagation is along the central axis of the tube." - Scavone.   

Mathematical modeling of irregular instrument bores is "well approximated" by a series of cylindrical and conical sections.  This modeling redistributes the volume of perturbations equally around the central axis of the air column.  While this approximation may satisfy theoreticians as to the perceived nature of reality, you will never convince a mouthpiece maker that a drastic enlargement in the side of the mouthpiece wall, resulting in a 2+ mm high, perpendicular obstruction upon entering the throat, is as efficient as an equivalent, though evenly distributed enlargement of the mouthpiece chamber around the central axis, resulting in a slight, smooth contraction of the bore upon entering the throat.  A 2+ mm deep
 enlargement in the side of the mouthpiece is equivalent to a closed tone hole.  Hmmm.

"Well, just remove the wall with a file.", you say.  As Morgan noticed, this removes the resistance caused by the ramp wall, but causes the mouthpiece to become less centered.   Small wonder.  The chamber is now larger, the width/length ratio is changed, and, if the mouthpiece volume/frequency was matched optimally to that particular instrument/player before, it's acoustic efficiency is now reduced.   You now have more volume in that area than you would like.  You have to start over and redesign the mouthpiece to get the original sound you wanted, without the resistance.  In the end, you will have a mouthpiece with as little cross-sectional window volume and obstruction as possible and that original window volume, distributed equally around the central axis of the chamber bore.

In this case, I would consider
 the saxophone mouthpiece makers to be the experts, not the theoreticians nor the shak makers, nor those who admire them.  There's nothing like having the actual, real experience.

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

> 

> > 

> 

> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in

> 

> >  the reed

> 

> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

> 

> > 

> 

> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> 

> > 

> 

> > 

> 

> > 

> 

> > --- On Sun, 6/12/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

> 

> > 

> 

> > From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>

> >     

> 

> >       

> 

> >       

> 

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

> 

> > 

> 

> > Toby

> >

> 

> >       We have ramps simply because the designer desires to have a table with a certain thickness.  One of the nicest sounding/playing pieces I ever had (after doing some chamber modifications), was a metal Colletto.  The throat bore entered the chamber at a very low angle, and the table wall was very, very thin at the window, and just thick enough at the shank, to handle the stress of holding the mouthpiece on the cork.  It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist. 

> 

> > 

> 

> > As Benade said, an unimportant part of the air column doesn't exist, meaning, every mm affects the sound/response of the instrument in some way.  IMO, having space at the end of the window/in front of a high ramp, on a mouthpiece with a thick table, is a mistake, and rounding/angling the ramp, while reducing the

> 

> >  obstacle, only

> 

> >  enlarges the perturbation.

> > > Nederveen describes the oscillative flow entering the instrument as being, "composed of the oscillative flow Us1 through the slit and the oscillative flow due to the movement of the reed.Â Â  U = Us1 - Sr dh/dt", where the reed movement is simply subtracted from and limits the flow, depending upon it's position.Â  That would not support Ferron's assumption.Â  Remember, during a regime, the reed motion is coupled to the air column's air pressure fluctuations.Â  The air pressure moves the reed.Â  Not visa versa. Then the reed valve position limits the flow.Â  No regime, the reed stays shut.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
When a neck suffers some degree of "pull down", it is reduced in volume 
to some extent where the bend occurred. This has measurable and 
significant effects.



The shape of the cross-section (circular, elliptical, triangular, squared, ...) do not matter.



The curvature of the neck has negligible effects (contrarily to the curvature of the elbow).

 


	
		
			
			
				
					 Originally Posted by Henry D
					
				
				On an ideal neck a cross section taken perpendicular to the center axis of the neck as it curves would be circular...


			
		
	
 No. A circular cross section is not acoustically superior to any 
other shape such as ellipsis or even squares, they will behave the same 
if they have the same area.



--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



  


    
      
      
      ...and, perhaps you would be kind enough to quote the context where Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence.  



--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.



--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@yahoo.com> wrote:

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

>

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@yahoo.com> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

> 

> >       You are right that reed channel effects would not directly influence things at the ramp, but I have yet to see any definitive numerical work on the mpc chamber, and what I have read is that things in that area are poorly understood.. As Scavone notes, changes in that area would tend to affect tone color, by influencing the impedances of higher harmonics. Wyman talks a lot about how the ramp angle changes response, and it is not clear why that should be so if we are simply talking about perturbation theory, since in that case only the cross-sectional volume should matter, as the bend in the air column would be insignificant acoustically. I wonder if there is some sort of reflection set up at the end of the window that affects the reed behavior.

> > 

> 

> > It might be interesting to make the window very short: starting just at the break, and make the ramp extremely

> 

> >  smooth and gradual. One could do so pretty easily using epoxy putty. If I had two identical mpcs I would give it a try, but with a single mpc, trying to gauge the before/after accurately would be difficult.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
That's fine, but Morgan and I were discussing the resistance inducing effect of a pronounced ramp wall/deep window, only 45mm (tenor) from the tip of the reed, and the effect of enlarging that perturbation even more by cutting down the wall in an effort to eliminate resistance. .  This process alters the character of the mouthpiece considerably, making it less centered/focused.  

I think it is universally agreed that in the mouthpiece chamber and baffle area, shape and volume distribution mean everything.  . 

If you disagree with what Morgan and I have experienced, and/or have some information that relates directly to ramp height and 
mouthpiece resistance it would be appreciated, but obscuring the issue, 
with unrelated facts (shak bores, unrelated quotes, etc.), just to 
disagree for the sake of disagreement, is of service to no-one.  Further, Antoine might not appreciate being quoted as you have done, seriously out of 
context, (i.e. "shape is of no consequence.:) where his statement is 
obviously incorrect.









--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 11:06 PM







 



  


    
      
      
      When a neck suffers some degree of "pull down", it is reduced in volume 
to some extent where the bend occurred. This has measurable and 
significant effects.



The shape of the cross-section (circular, elliptical, triangular, squared, ...) do not matter.



The curvature of the neck has negligible effects (contrarily to the curvature of the elbow).

 


	
		
			
			
				
					 Originally Posted by Henry D
					
				
				On an ideal neck a cross section taken perpendicular to the center axis of the neck as it curves would be circular...


			
		
	
 No. A circular cross section is not acoustically superior to any 
other shape such as ellipsis or even squares, they will behave the same 
if they have the same area.



--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      ...and, perhaps you would be kind enough to quote the context where Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence.  



--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.



--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

> >       You are right that reed channel effects would not directly influence things at the ramp, but I have yet to see any definitive numerical work on the mpc chamber, and what I have read is that things in that area are poorly understood.. As Scavone notes, changes in that area would tend to affect tone color, by influencing the impedances of higher harmonics. Wyman talks a lot about how the ramp angle changes response, and it is not clear why that should be so if we are simply talking about perturbation theory, since in that case only the cross-sectional volume should matter, as the bend in the air column would be insignificant acoustically. I wonder if there is some sort of reflection set up at the end of the window that affects the reed behavior.

>

>

> 

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

> 

> > 

> 

> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in

> 

> >  the reed

> 

> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

> 

> > 

> 

> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> 

> >

> 

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

> 

> > 

> 

> > Toby

> 

> >       We have ramps simply because the designer desires to have a table with a certain thickness.  One of the nicest sounding/playing pieces I ever had (after doing some chamber modifications), was a metal Colletto.  The throat bore entered the chamber at a very low angle, and the table wall was very, very thin at the window, and just thick enough at the shank, to handle the stress of holding the mouthpiece on the cork.  It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist. 

> 

> > 

> 

> > As Benade said, an unimportant part of the air column doesn't exist, meaning, every mm affects the sound/response of the instrument in some way.  IMO, having space at the end of the window/in front of a high ramp, on a mouthpiece with a thick table, is a mistake, and rounding/angling the ramp, while reducing the

> 

> >  obstacle, only

> 

> >  enlarges the perturbation.

Then the reed valve position limits the flow.Â  No regime, the reed stays shut.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
This is from the thread about a pulled down neck. It might be useful to add one more part of the quote:

"This is actually the case. Saxophone necks have non-cylindrical cross 
sections where they are bent. Some years ago, I unsoldered all parts 
from an alto neck (Selmer Series II, never pulled down) to measure the 
dimensions:


as an example, the external diameters, as measured top-down versus 
right-left, for one cross-section are 19.5mm diameter and 20.4mm 
diameter. The actual shape of the cross-section is probably not an 
ellipse but some non-circular shape, more or less symmetric, a bit 
egg-shaped or something else, as are most real things, non-ideal.



This does not matter, what matters is the cross-sectional area as a function of the distance along the spine of the instrument. " (my italics)

The "bend" caused by the offset of the volume resulting from a deep window is small, akin to the bend in the neck. It should not matter. If your experience tells you that there is a difference between a deep and shallow ramp--and the volume is really, truly equal--you must look elsewhere for the explanation.

> 

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

> 

> > 

> 

> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in

> 

> >  the reed

> 

> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

> 

> > 

> 

> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> 

> > 

> 

> > 

> 

> >

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
You are not following very well.  

The wall is the resistance problem.  It is caused by a lopsided volume distribution.  Distribute the volume evenly and there is no wall and no resistance problem.  Trying to eliminate the wall by cutting down an existing wall creates a new, larger chamber that is less centered or unfocused.  This is unacceptable in most cases.  Filling the window in, up to the break, is a good solution, but the volume loss must be made up by removing material elsewhere - the volume then, is more evenly distributed which brings us to the best solution, which is to make the mouthpiece without a lopsided volume distribution in the first place.



--- On Wed, 6/15/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wednesday, June 15, 2011, 1:59 AM







 



  


    
      
      
      This is from the thread about a pulled down neck. It might be useful to add one more part of the quote:

"This is actually the case. Saxophone necks have non-cylindrical cross 
sections where they are bent. Some years ago, I unsoldered all parts 
from an alto neck (Selmer Series II, never pulled down) to measure the 
dimensions:


as an example, the external diameters, as measured top-down versus 
right-left, for one cross-section are 19.5mm diameter and 20.4mm 
diameter. The actual shape of the cross-section is probably not an 
ellipse but some non-circular shape, more or less symmetric, a bit 
egg-shaped or something else, as are most real things, non-ideal.



This does not matter, what matters is the cross-sectional area as a function of the distance along the spine of the instrument. " (my italics)

The "bend" caused by the offset of the volume resulting from a deep window is small, akin to the bend in the neck. It should not matter. If your experience tells you that there is a difference between a deep and shallow ramp--and the volume is really, truly equal--you must look elsewhere for the explanation.

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      When a neck suffers some degree of "pull down", it is reduced in volume 
to some extent where the bend occurred. This has measurable and 
significant effects.



The shape of the cross-section (circular, elliptical, triangular, squared, ...) do not matter.



The curvature of the neck has negligible effects (contrarily to the curvature of the elbow).

 


	
		
			
			
				
					 Originally Posted by Henry D
					
				
				On an ideal neck a cross section taken perpendicular to the center axis of the neck as it curves would be circular...


			
		
	
 No. A circular cross section is not acoustically superior to any 
other shape such as ellipsis or even squares, they will behave the same 
if they have the same area.



--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      ...and, perhaps you would be kind enough to quote the context where Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence.  



--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@ybb.ne.jp <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.



--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
What evidence do you have that the "wall" (it is more a bulge) creates resistance, if it is not sharp enough to create a reflection or turbulence?

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



  


    
      
      
      You are not following very well.  

The wall is the resistance problem.  It is caused by a lopsided volume distribution.  Distribute the volume evenly and there is no wall and no resistance problem.  Trying to eliminate the wall by cutting down an existing wall creates a new, larger chamber that is less centered or unfocused.  This is unacceptable in most cases.  Filling the window in, up to the break, is a good solution, but the volume loss must be made up by removing material elsewhere - the volume then, is more evenly distributed which brings us to the best solution, which is to make the mouthpiece without a lopsided volume distribution in the first place.



--- On Wed, 6/15/11, kymarto123@....jp <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@ybb.ne.jp>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wednesday, June 15, 2011, 1:59 AM







 



    
      
      
      This is from the thread about a pulled down neck. It might be useful to add one more part of the quote:

"This is actually the case. Saxophone necks have non-cylindrical cross 
sections where they are bent. Some years ago, I unsoldered all parts 
from an alto neck (Selmer Series II, never pulled down) to measure the 
dimensions:


as an example, the external diameters, as measured top-down versus 
right-left, for one cross-section are 19.5mm diameter and 20.4mm 
diameter. The actual shape of the cross-section is probably not an 
ellipse but some non-circular shape, more or less symmetric, a bit 
egg-shaped or something else, as are most real things, non-ideal.



This does not matter, what matters is the cross-sectional area as a function of the distance along the spine of the instrument. " (my italics)

The "bend" caused by the offset of the volume resulting from a deep window is small, akin to the bend in the neck. It should not matter. If your experience tells you that there is a difference between a deep and shallow ramp--and the volume is really, truly equal--you must look elsewhere for the explanation.

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      When a neck suffers some degree of "pull down", it is reduced in volume 
to some extent where the bend occurred. This has measurable and 
significant effects.



The shape of the cross-section (circular, elliptical, triangular, squared, ...) do not matter.



The curvature of the neck has negligible effects (contrarily to the curvature of the elbow).

 


	
		
			
			
				
					 Originally Posted by Henry D
					
				
				On an ideal neck a cross section taken perpendicular to the center axis of the neck as it curves would be circular...


			
		
	
 No. A circular cross section is not acoustically superior to any 
other shape such as ellipsis or even squares, they will behave the same 
if they have the same area.



--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      ...and, perhaps you would be kind enough to quote the context where Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence.  



--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.



--- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

> >       You are right that reed channel effects would not directly influence things at the ramp, but I have yet to see any definitive numerical work on the mpc chamber, and what I have read is that things in that area are poorly understood.. > 

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

> 

> > 

> 

> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in

> 

> >  the reed

> 

> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

> 

> > 

> 

> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> 

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist.

C'mon Toby.  Look at a few mouthpieces.  Some have a straight, vertical wall of 2+mm.   Besides, it is well documented that any sudden change in bore diameter causes a change in air column impedance and a partial reflection.

> If the physicists' first order approximation (the grossest simplification of reality that has any useful validity) were sufficient to satisfy any musician, then you could convince them that mouthpiece shape (short and fat vs. long and slender) has no significance - since accordingly, all mouthpieces are the same.

> 

> If the mouthpiece designer actually wanted that much cross-sectional volume at the end of the window, it would be acoustically more efficient to distribute that volume equally, around the central axis of the mouthpiece bore, rather than to lump it all on one side, resulting in a lopsided air column shape and a bore obstruction - in the mouthpiece already.  

> 

> I don't know that much about shakuhachi flutes, but to adopt an argument style I hear often, "If shaks are so good, why isn't Yamaha making saxophones that way?" :-)

> --- MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> 

> > Make a rubber or clay model of the mouthpiece chamber, including the baffle, window, ramp, throat, etc..  Looking at the shape of the mouthpiece air column, it's easy to see how every contour and curve could function acoustically - except the lopsided, lump of a window, with an abrupt, thick, ramp wall at the end.  That chamber volume could easily be more effectively distributed around the entire chamber circumference, if needed at that distance from the reed.  This is an example of structural considerations (table wall strength) preempting those acoustical.

> >       If you read this paper a little more closely, you'll see that "near the reed" is defined by Gilbert, et al., as the "reed channel", which, "...consists of two parts: a) the front slit delimited by the edge of the mouthpiece tip (baffle) and the reed, and b) the lateral slits between the lay (curved wall against which the reed is pressed) and the reed.  We will further assume that the reed channel geometry is determined  by the front reed channel height hr at the tip of the reed."

> 

> > 

> 

> > Further: "Typically the reed channel length Lr is in the order of one millimeter. For long reed channels (Lr/hr > 4) the viscous entrainment by the jet in

> 

> >  the reed

> 

> >  channel induces a reattachment of the flow to the walls."

> 

> > 

> 

> > This is all very interesting, but we are discussing the ramp area, which is I'd say, in the scope of Gilbert's paper, nowhere near the reed channel.  Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> 

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

>

> 

> > Toby

> >       We have ramps simply because the designer desires to have a table with a certain thickness.  One of the nicest sounding/playing pieces I ever had (after doing some chamber modifications), was a metal Colletto.  The throat bore entered the chamber at a very low angle, and the table wall was very, very thin at the window, and just thick enough at the shank, to handle the stress of holding the mouthpiece on the cork.  It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist.

Then the reed valve position limits the flow.Â  No regime, the reed stays shut.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
Agreed; I'm certainly not talking about a straight wall with a sharp edge. But there are plenty of mpcs that have a rounded curve back from the window. Of course, the real way to test all this would be to take identical mpcs and create different profiles at the window. Certainly everything, as you say, has an effect to some degree. A partial reflection might well explain why a gradual ramp would react differently than a "wall", but in my understanding, the distribution of the volume, if there are no sudden changes in bore diameter, does not matter. That has been the point all along in my argument.

It seems to me that with appropriate contouring at the back of the window cutout, and perhaps a bit of material added in the chamber at that position to compensate if necessary, it would be entirely possible to get the same effect as cutting down the table, without the possibility of introducing structural instability in the mpc (especially if it is not metal). The fact that the volume is still off-center towards the reed should not matter if there is no abrupt change or edge, which was the point that I have been trying to make.

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



  


    
      
      
      C'mon Toby.  Look at a few mouthpieces.  Some have a straight, vertical wall of 2+mm.   Besides, it is well documented that any sudden change in bore diameter causes a change in air column impedance and a partial reflection.

--- On Wed, 6/15/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wednesday, June 15, 2011, 6:05 AM







 



    
      
      
      What evidence do you have that the "wall" (it is more a bulge) creates resistance, if it is not sharp enough to create a reflection or turbulence?

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      You are not following very well.  

The wall is the resistance problem.  It is caused by a lopsided volume distribution.  Distribute the volume evenly and there is no wall and no resistance problem.  Trying to eliminate the wall by cutting down an existing wall creates a new, larger chamber that is less centered or unfocused.  This is unacceptable in most cases.  Filling the window in, up to the break, is a good solution, but the volume loss must be made up by removing material elsewhere - the volume then, is more evenly distributed which brings us to the best solution, which is to make the mouthpiece without a lopsided volume distribution in the first place.



--- On Wed, 6/15/11, kymarto123@ybb.ne.jp <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@ybb.ne.jp>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wednesday, June 15, 2011, 1:59 AM







 



    
      
      
      This is from the thread about a pulled down neck. It might be useful to add one more part of the quote:

"This is actually the case. Saxophone necks have non-cylindrical cross 
sections where they are bent. Some years ago, I unsoldered all parts 
from an alto neck (Selmer Series II, never pulled down) to measure the 
dimensions:


as an example, the external diameters, as measured top-down versus 
right-left, for one cross-section are 19.5mm diameter and 20.4mm 
diameter. The actual shape of the cross-section is probably not an 
ellipse but some non-circular shape, more or less symmetric, a bit 
egg-shaped or something else, as are most real things, non-ideal.



This does not matter, what matters is the cross-sectional area as a function of the distance along the spine of the instrument. " (my italics)

The "bend" caused by the offset of the volume resulting from a deep window is small, akin to the bend in the neck. It should not matter. If your experience tells you that there is a difference between a deep and shallow ramp--and the volume is really, truly equal--you must look elsewhere for the explanation.

--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      When a neck suffers some degree of "pull down", it is reduced in volume 
to some extent where the bend occurred. This has measurable and 
significant effects.



The shape of the cross-section (circular, elliptical, triangular, squared, ...) do not matter.



The curvature of the neck has negligible effects (contrarily to the curvature of the elbow).

 


	
		
			
			
				
					 Originally Posted by Henry D
					
				
				On an ideal neck a cross section taken perpendicular to the center axis of the neck as it curves would be circular...


			
		
	
 No. A circular cross section is not acoustically superior to any 
other shape such as ellipsis or even squares, they will behave the same 
if they have the same area.



--- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:















 
 



    
      
      
      ...and, perhaps you would be kind enough to quote the context where Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence.  



--- On Tue, 6/14/11, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re:Re:Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Effects of ramp height
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tuesday, June 14, 2011, 12:46 PM







 



    
      
      
      Sorry, doesn't compute for me. By what acoustic principle should the volume be distributed equally around the central axis? You might have noted on SOTW that Antoine Lefebvre mentioned that shape is of no consequence. 



Of course volume as a function of length IS of consequence, which helps to explain the differences between short/fat and long/narrow. 



If there is a perturbation of a nature to cause turbulence or a reflection at that point it is a different matter, but the shape of the volume, within fairly wide limits, doesn't appear to matter, at least according to the experts.



There is no point in deliberately making an air column relatively bumpy, but the point was that a bumpy air column can function as well as a smooth one. 



John Neptune, one of Japan's top shak players and makers, let me play his main flute at one workshop. Amazing beast, it was. I was totally flabbergasted when I sighted down it and saw that it has the most uneven bore I had ever seen, with scoops and dollops of putty everywhere. He is a master of adjustment, and had been tinkering with it for years, doing large and small local volume adjustments everywhere. That certainly convinced me that Antoine is quite right.

Clearly, past the break, where the reed never vibrates, the flow has reattached itself to the walls of the mouthpiece, and we can assume that "simple" perturbation theory applies.  My personal experience in manipulating ramp geometry would substantiate this, at least for myself.

> 

> >       If you read the paper on the aero-acoustics of the clarinet, you'll see that what happens near the reed is extremely complex and poorly understood. We are no longer talking here about simple perturbation theory, but the behavior of the free jet caused by the reed oscillation, and how it separates from the reed channel and interacts with the rest of the air column, as well as many other aero-acoustic parameter. No one seems to have a very good clue for the modeling of what is happening around there.

>

> 

> > Toby

> >       We have ramps simply because the designer desires to have a table with a certain thickness.  One of the nicest sounding/playing pieces I ever had (after doing some chamber modifications), was a metal Colletto.  The throat bore entered the chamber at a very low angle, and the table wall was very, very thin at the window, and just thick enough at the shank, to handle the stress of holding the mouthpiece on the cork.  It was balanced, free-blowing, and centered.   A ramp effectively didn't exist. 

> 

> > 

> 

> > As Benade said, an unimportant part of the air column doesn't exist, meaning, every mm affects the sound/response of the instrument in some way.  IMO, having space at the end of the window/in front of a high ramp, on a mouthpiece with a thick table, is a mistake, and rounding/angling the ramp, while reducing the

> 

> >  obstacle, only

> 

> >  enlarges the perturbation.

> > > Nederveen describes the oscillative flow entering the instrument as being, "composed of the oscillative flow Us1 through the slit and the oscillative flow due to the movement of the reed.Â Â  U = Us1 - Sr dh/dt", where the reed movement is simply subtracted from and limits the flow, depending upon it's position.Â  That would not support Ferron's assumption.Â  Remember, during a regime, the reed motion is coupled to the air column's air pressure fluctuations.Â  The air pressure moves the reed.Â  Not visa versa. Then the reed valve position limits the flow.Â  No regime, the reed stays shut.

FROM: frymorgan (Morgan)
SUBJECT: Re: Effects of ramp height
Experience is evidence enough.  I see it just about every day.  I'm not really interested in debating whether or not this is real, my observations are enough to convince me and they are consistent with the observations of others who reface mouthpieces. 

What I am interested in is why this happens.
 

--- In MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com, kymarto123@... wrote:
>
> What evidence do you have that the "wall" (it is more a bulge) creates resistance, if it is not sharp enough to create a reflection or turbulence?
> 
> --- On Wed, 2011/6/15, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
> 
>  
>  
> 
> 
> 
>   
> 
> 
>     
>       
>       
>       You are not following very well.  
> 
> The wall is the resistance problem.  It is caused by a lopsided volume distribution.  Distribute the volume evenly and there is no wall and no resistance problem.  Trying to eliminate the wall by cutting down an existing wall creates a new, larger chamber that is less centered or unfocused.  This is unacceptable in most cases.  Filling the window in, up to the break, is a good solution, but the volume loss must be made up by removing material elsewhere - the volume then, is more evenly distributed which brings us to the best solution, which is to make the mouthpiece without a lopsided volume distribution in the first place.
> 
> 
>
